<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">State of geopolitical tension after World War II between powers in the Eastern Bloc and the Western Bloc</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the state of political tension in the 20th century. For the general term, see <a href="/wiki/Cold_war_(general_term)" title="Cold war (general term)">Cold war (general term)</a>. For the current state of political tension, see <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_II" title="Cold War II">Cold War II</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Cold_War_(disambiguation)" title="Cold War (disambiguation)">Cold War (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Cold warrior" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Cold_warrior_(disambiguation)" title="Cold warrior (disambiguation)">Cold warrior (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p (1947&#8211;1991)</div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berlin_Wall_1961-11-20.jpg"><img alt="" one of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_weapons_tests_of_the_United_States" title="List of nuclear weapons tests of the United States">more than a thousand</a> such tests conducted by the US between 1945 and 1992</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="661" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg/250px-West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg/375px-West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg/500px-West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The fall of the Berlin Wall, 1989</div></div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>Cold War</b> was a period of geopolitical tension between the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> with <a href="/wiki/Satellite_state#Post-World_War_II" title="Satellite state">its satellite states</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> with its allies (the <a href="/wiki/Western_Bloc" title="Western Bloc">Western Bloc</a>) after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. A common <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_the_Cold_War" title="Historiography of the Cold War">historiography of the conflict</a> begins with 1946, the year U.S. diplomat <a href="/wiki/George_F._Kennan" title="George F. Kennan">George F. Kennan</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/X_Article" title="X Article">Long Telegram</a>" from <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> cemented a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_United_States" title="Foreign policy of the United States">U.S. foreign policy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Containment" title="Containment">containment</a> of Soviet <a href="/wiki/World_communism" title="World communism">expansionism</a> threatening strategically vital regions, and ending between the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989" title="Revolutions of 1989">Revolutions of 1989</a> and the 1991 <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">collapse of the USSR</a>, which ended <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>. The term "<a href="/wiki/Cold_war_(general_term)" title="Cold war (general term)">cold</a>" is used because there was no large-scale fighting directly between the two sides, but they each supported major regional wars known as <a href="/wiki/Proxy_war" title="Proxy war">proxy wars</a>.\n</p><p>The Cold War split the temporary <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">wartime alliance</a> against <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, leaving the Soviet Union and the United States as two <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpowers</a> with profound economic and political differences. The USSR was a <a href="/wiki/Marxism%E2%80%93Leninism" title="Marxism&#8211;Leninism">Marxist&#8211;Leninist</a> state led by its <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Communist Party of the Soviet Union">Communist Party</a>, which in turn was dominated by a <a href="/wiki/List_of_leaders_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="List of leaders of the Soviet Union">leader</a> with different titles over time, and a small committee called the <a href="/wiki/Politburo_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Politburo of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union">Politburo</a>. The Party controlled the state, the press, the military, the economy, and many organizations throughout the <a href="/wiki/Second_World" title="Second World">Second World</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> and other satellites, and funded communist parties around the world, sometimes in competition with communist <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, particularly following the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">Sino-Soviet split</a> of the 1960s. In opposition stood the <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist</a> West, led by the United States, a <a href="/wiki/Federal_republic" title="Federal republic">federal republic</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Two-party_system" title="Two-party system">two-party</a> <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential system</a>. The <a href="/wiki/First_World" title="First World">First World</a> nations of the Western Bloc were generally <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democratic</a> with a free press and independent organizations, but were economically and politically entwined with a network of <a href="/wiki/Banana_republic" title="Banana republic">banana republics</a> and other authoritarian regimes throughout the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a>, most of which were the Western Bloc\'s <a href="/wiki/Western_European_colonialism_and_colonization" title="Western European colonialism and colonization">former colonies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Some major Cold War frontlines such as <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Congo</a> were still Western colonies in 1947.\n</p><p>A neutral bloc arose with the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>, which sought good relations with both sides. Notwithstanding relatively isolated incidents of <a href="/wiki/Air-to-air_combat_losses_between_the_Soviet_Union_and_the_United_States" title="Air-to-air combat losses between the Soviet Union and the United States">air-to-air dogfights</a> and shoot-downs, the two superpowers never engaged directly in full-scale armed combat. However, both were heavily armed in preparation for a possible all-out <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">nuclear world war</a>. Each side had a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_strategy" title="Nuclear strategy">nuclear strategy</a> that discouraged <a href="/wiki/Pre-emptive_nuclear_strike" title="Pre-emptive nuclear strike">an attack</a> by the other side, on the basis that such an attack would <a href="/wiki/Second_strike" title="Second strike">lead to the total destruction of the attacker</a>&#8212;the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_assured_destruction" title="Mutual assured destruction">mutually assured destruction</a> (MAD). Aside from the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_arms_race" title="Nuclear arms race">development of the two sides\' nuclear arsenals</a>, and their deployment of conventional military forces, the struggle for dominance was expressed via proxy wars around the globe, <a href="/wiki/Psychological_warfare" title="Psychological warfare">psychological warfare</a>, massive propaganda campaigns and <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_espionage" title="Cold War espionage">espionage</a>, far-reaching <a href="/wiki/Economic_sanctions" title="Economic sanctions">embargos</a>, rivalry at sports events, and technological competitions such as the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1947%E2%80%931953)" title="Cold War (1947&#8211;1953)">The first phase</a> of the Cold War began in the first two years after the end of the Second World War in 1945. The USSR consolidated its control over the states of the Eastern Bloc, while the United States began a strategy of global containment to challenge Soviet power, extending military and financial aid to the countries of Western Europe (for example, supporting the anti-communist side in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Civil_War" title="Greek Civil War">Greek Civil War</a>) and <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty" title="North Atlantic Treaty">creating</a> the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO alliance</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Blockade" title="Berlin Blockade">Berlin Blockade</a> (1948&#8211;49) was the first major crisis of the Cold War. With the victory of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist side</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> and the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> (1950&#8211;53), <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1953%E2%80%931962)" title="Cold War (1953&#8211;1962)">the conflict expanded</a>. The USSR and the US competed for influence in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Decolonization" title="Decolonization">decolonizing</a> states of Africa and Asia. The Soviets suppressed the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956" title="Hungarian Revolution of 1956">Hungarian Revolution of 1956</a>. The expansion and escalation sparked more crises, such as the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a> (1956), the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Crisis_of_1961" title="Berlin Crisis of 1961">Berlin Crisis of 1961</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a> of 1962, which was perhaps the closest the two sides came to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">nuclear war</a>. Meanwhile, an international <a href="/wiki/Peace_movement" title="Peace movement">peace movement</a> took root and grew among citizens around the world, first in Japan from 1954, when people became concerned about <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_testing" title="Nuclear weapons testing">nuclear weapons testing</a>, but soon also in Europe and the US. The peace movement, and in particular the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_anti-nuclear_movement" title="History of the anti-nuclear movement">anti-nuclear movement</a>, gained pace and popularity from the late 1950s and early 1960s, and continued to grow through the \'70s and \'80s with large protest marches, demonstrations, and various non-parliamentary activism opposing war and calling for global <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_disarmament" title="Nuclear disarmament">nuclear disarmament</a>. Following the Cuban Missile Crisis, <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1962%E2%80%931979)" title="Cold War (1962&#8211;1979)">a new phase began</a> that saw the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">Sino-Soviet split</a> complicate relations within the Communist sphere, while US allies, particularly <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, demonstrated greater independence of action. The USSR <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact_invasion_of_Czechoslovakia" title="Warsaw Pact invasion of Czechoslovakia">crushed</a> the 1968 <a href="/wiki/Prague_Spring" title="Prague Spring">Prague Spring</a> liberalization program in <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovak_Socialist_Republic" title="Czechoslovak Socialist Republic">Czechoslovakia</a>, while the US experienced internal turmoil from the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Opposition_to_United_States_involvement_in_the_Vietnam_War" title="Opposition to United States involvement in the Vietnam War">opposition</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> (1955&#8211;75), which ended with the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Saigon" title="Fall of Saigon">defeat</a> of the US-backed <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">Republic of Vietnam</a>, prompting further adjustments.\n</p><p>By the 1970s, both sides had become interested in making allowances in order to create a more stable and predictable international system, ushering in a period of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9tente" title="D&#233;tente">d&#233;tente</a> that saw <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Arms_Limitation_Talks" title="Strategic Arms Limitation Talks">Strategic Arms Limitation Talks</a> and the US <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon%27s_1972_visit_to_China" title="Richard Nixon\'s 1972 visit to China">opening relations</a> with the People\'s Republic of China as a strategic counterweight to the Soviet Union. D&#233;tente collapsed at the end of the decade with the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War" title="Soviet&#8211;Afghan War">Soviet&#8211;Afghan War</a> in 1979. The <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1979%E2%80%931985)" title="Cold War (1979&#8211;1985)">early 1980s</a> were another period of elevated tension, with the Soviet downing of <a href="/wiki/Korean_Air_Lines_Flight_007" title="Korean Air Lines Flight 007">KAL Flight 007</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Able_Archer_83" title="Able Archer 83">"Able Archer" NATO military exercises</a>, both in 1983. The United States increased <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Doctrine" title="Reagan Doctrine">diplomatic, military, and economic pressures</a> on the Soviet Union, at a time when the communist state was already suffering from <a href="/wiki/Era_of_Stagnation" title="Era of Stagnation">economic stagnation</a>. On 12 June 1982, a million protesters gathered in <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">Central Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> to call for an end to the Cold War arms race and nuclear weapons in particular. <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1985%E2%80%931991)" title="Cold War (1985&#8211;1991)">In the mid-1980s</a>, the new Soviet leader <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a> introduced the liberalizing reforms of <i><a href="/wiki/Perestroika" title="Perestroika">perestroika</a></i> ("reorganization", 1987) and <i><a href="/wiki/Glasnost" title="Glasnost">glasnost</a></i> ("openness", c. 1985) and ended Soviet involvement in Afghanistan. Pressures for national independence grew stronger in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Polish_People%27s_Republic" title="Polish People\'s Republic">Poland</a>. Gorbachev meanwhile refused to use Soviet troops to bolster the faltering <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> regimes as had occurred in the past. The result in 1989 was <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989" title="Revolutions of 1989">a wave of revolutions</a> that peacefully (with the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Revolution" title="Romanian Revolution">Romanian Revolution</a>) overthrew all of the communist regimes of Central and Eastern Europe. The Communist Party of the Soviet Union itself lost control and was banned following an <a href="/wiki/1991_Soviet_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1991 Soviet coup d\'&#233;tat attempt">abortive coup attempt</a> in August 1991. This in turn led to the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">formal dissolution of the USSR</a> in December 1991 and the collapse of communist regimes in other countries such as <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_People%27s_Republic" title="Mongolian People\'s Republic">Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Kampuchea" title="People\'s Republic of Kampuchea">Cambodia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_Yemen" title="South Yemen">South Yemen</a>. The United States remained as the world\'s only superpower.\n</p><p>The Cold War and its events have left a significant legacy. It is often referred to <a href="/wiki/Culture_during_the_Cold_War" title="Culture during the Cold War">in popular culture</a>, especially in media featuring themes of <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">espionage</a> (notably the internationally successful <i><a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">James Bond</a></i> book and film franchise) and the threat of nuclear warfare. Meanwhile, a renewed state of tension between the Soviet Union\'s successor state, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, and the United States in the 2010s (including its Western allies) has been referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_II" title="Cold War II">Second Cold War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guterres_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guterres-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_the_term">Origins of the term</span></h2>\n<div (1985&#8211;1991)</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="padding:0.2em 0.5em; background:#ccf;"><a href="/wiki/Frozen_conflict" title="Frozen conflict">Frozen conflicts</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_events_in_the_Cold_War" title="Timeline of events in the Cold War">Timeline</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/List_of_conflicts_related_to_the_Cold_War" title="List of conflicts related to the Cold War">Conflicts</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_the_Cold_War" title="Historiography of the Cold War">Historiography</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="padding:0.2em 0.5em; background:#ccf;"><a href="/wiki/Cold_War_II" title="Cold War II">Cold War II</a>\n</th></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p>At the end of World War II, English writer <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> used <i><a href="/wiki/Cold_war_(general_term)" title="Cold war (general term)">cold war</a></i>, as a general term, in his essay "You and the Atomic Bomb", published 19 October 1945 in the British newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Tribune_(magazine)" title="Tribune (magazine)">Tribune</a></i>. Contemplating a world living in the shadow of the threat of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">nuclear warfare</a>, Orwell looked at <a href="/wiki/James_Burnham" title="James Burnham">James Burnham</a>\'s predictions of a polarized world, writing:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Looking at the world as a whole, the drift for many decades has been not towards anarchy but towards the reimposition of slavery... James Burnham\'s theory has been much discussed, but few people have yet considered its ideological implications&#8212;that is, the kind of world-view, the kind of beliefs, and the social structure that would probably prevail in a state which was at once unconquerable and in a permanent state of "cold war" with its neighbours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> of 10 March 1946, Orwell wrote, "after the Moscow conference last December, Russia began to make a \'cold war\' on Britain and the British Empire."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first use of the term to describe the specific <a href="/wiki/Post-war" title="Post-war">post-war</a> geopolitical confrontation between the Soviet Union and the United States came in a speech by <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Baruch" title="Bernard Baruch">Bernard Baruch</a>, an influential advisor to Democratic presidents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> on 16 April 1947. The speech, written by journalist <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Bayard_Swope" title="Herbert Bayard Swope">Herbert Bayard Swope</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> proclaimed, "Let us not be deceived: we are today in the midst of a cold war."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Newspaper columnist <a href="/wiki/Walter_Lippmann" title="Walter Lippmann">Walter Lippmann</a> gave the term wide currency with his book <i>The Cold War</i>. When asked in 1947 about the source of the term, Lippmann traced it to a French term from the 1930s, <i>la guerre froide</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_Cold_War" title="Origins of the Cold War">Origins of the Cold War</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_Revolution">Russian Revolution</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wladiwostok_Parade_1918.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2261" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Wladiwostok_Parade_1918.jpg/220px-Wladiwostok_Parade_1918.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Wladiwostok_Parade_1918.jpg/330px-Wladiwostok_Parade_1918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Wladiwostok_Parade_1918.jpg/440px-Wladiwostok_Parade_1918.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wladiwostok_Parade_1918.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Allied troops in <a href="/wiki/Vladivostok" title="Vladivostok">Vladivostok</a>, August 1918, during the <a href="/wiki/Allied_intervention_in_the_Russian_Civil_War" title="Allied intervention in the Russian Civil War">Allied intervention</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">Russian Civil War</a></div></div></div>\n<p>While most historians trace the origins of the Cold War to the period immediately following World War II, others argue that it began with the <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">October Revolution</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russian_Republic" title="Russian Republic">Russia</a> in 1917 when the <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolsheviks</a> took power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis-10">[10]</a></sup> In 1919 <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> stated that his new state was surrounded by a "hostile capitalist encirclement", and he viewed diplomacy as a weapon that should be used to keep the Soviet Union\'s enemies divided, beginning with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_International" title="Communist International">Communist International</a>, which called for revolutionary upheavals abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palmieri34_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palmieri34-11">[11]</a></sup> Historian Max Beloff argues that the Soviets saw "no prospect of permanent peace", with the 1922 Soviet Constitution proclaiming:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Since the time of the formation of the soviet republics, the states of the world have divided into two camps: the camp of capitalism and the camp of socialism. There&#8212;in the camp of capitalism&#8212;national enmity and inequality, colonial slavery, and chauvinism, national oppression and pogroms, imperialist brutalities and wars. Here&#8212;in the camp of socialism&#8212;mutual confidence and peace, national freedom and equality, a dwelling together in peace and the brotherly collaboration of peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>According to British historian Christopher Sutton:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In what some have called the First Cold War, from Britain\'s intervention in the Russian Civil War in 1918 to its uneasy alliance with the Soviet Union against the Axis powers in 1941, British distrust of the revolutionary and regicidal Bolsheviks resulted in domestic, foreign, and colonial policies aimed at resisting the spread of communism. This conflict after 1945 took on new battlefields, new weapons, new players, and a greater intensity, but it was still fundamentally a conflict against Soviet imperialism (real and imagined).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The idea of long-term continuity is a minority scholarly view that has been challenged. Frank Ninkovich writes:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As for the two cold wars thesis, the chief problem is that the two periods are incommensurable. To be sure, they were joined together by enduring ideological hostility, but in the post-World War I years Bolshevism was not a geopolitical menace. After World War II, in contrast, the Soviet Union was a superpower that combined ideological antagonism with the kind of geopolitical threat posed by Germany and Japan in the Second World War. Even with more amicable relations in the 1920s, it is conceivable that post-1945 relations would have turned out much the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>By 1933, old fears of Communist threats had faded, and the American business community, as well as newspaper editors, were calling for diplomatic recognition. President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> used presidential authority to normalize relations in November 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007341&#8211;343_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007341&#8211;343-15">[15]</a></sup> There were few complaints about the move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> However, there was no progress on the debt issue and little additional trade. Historians Justus D. Doenecke and Mark A. Stoler note that, "Both nations were soon disillusioned by the accord."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Many American businessmen expected a bonus in terms of large-scale trade, but it never materialized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Soviets had promised not to engage in spying inside the United States, but did so anyhow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (January 2019)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Roosevelt named <a href="/wiki/William_Christian_Bullitt_Jr." title="William Christian Bullitt Jr.">William Bullitt</a> as ambassador from 1933 to 1936. Bullitt arrived in Moscow with high hopes for Soviet&#8211;American relations, but his view of the Soviet leadership soured on closer inspection. By the end of his tenure, Bullitt was openly hostile to the Soviet government, and he remained an outspoken anti-communist for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginnings_of_World_War_II">Beginnings of World War II</span></h3>\n<p>In the late 1930s, Stalin had worked with Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Maxim_Litvinov" title="Maxim Litvinov">Maxim Litvinov</a> to promote <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Popular_fronts" title="Popular fronts">popular fronts</a> with capitalist parties and governments to oppose <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a>. The Soviets were embittered when Western governments chose to practice <a href="/wiki/Appeasement" title="Appeasement">appeasement</a> with Nazi Germany instead. In March 1939 Britain and France&#8212;without consulting the USSR&#8212;granted Hitler control of much of Czechoslovakia at the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">Munich Agreement</a>. Facing an aggressive Japan at Russia&#8217;s borders as well, Stalin changed directions and replaced Litvinov with <a href="/wiki/Vyacheslav_Molotov" title="Vyacheslav Molotov">Vyacheslav Molotov</a>, who negotiated closer relations with Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeffler200818-19_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeffler200818-19-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After signing the <a href="/wiki/Molotov%E2%80%93Ribbentrop_Pact" title="Molotov&#8211;Ribbentrop Pact">Molotov&#8211;Ribbentrop Pact</a> and <a href="/wiki/German%E2%80%93Soviet_Frontier_Treaty" title="German&#8211;Soviet Frontier Treaty">German&#8211;Soviet Frontier Treaty</a>, the Soviet Union forced the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic countries</a>&#8212;Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania&#8212;to allow it <a href="/wiki/Background_of_the_occupation_of_the_Baltic_states#Soviet_ultimatums_and_occupation" title="Background of the occupation of the Baltic states">to station Soviet troops in their countries</a> under pacts of "mutual assistance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmithPabriksPursLane200224_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmithPabriksPursLane200224-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBilinsky19999_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBilinsky19999-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett200155&#8211;56_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett200155&#8211;56-24">[24]</a></sup> Finland rejected territorial demands, prompting a Soviet invasion in November 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpring1986207&#8211;26_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpring1986207&#8211;26-25">[25]</a></sup> The resulting <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">Winter War</a> ended in March 1940 with <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Peace_Treaty" title="Moscow Peace Treaty">Finnish concessions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanhim&#228;ki199712_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanhim&#228;ki199712-26">[26]</a></sup> Britain and France, treating the Soviet attack on Finland as tantamount to its entering the war on the side of the Germans, responded to the Soviet invasion by supporting the USSR\'s expulsion from the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett200155&#8211;56_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett200155&#8211;56-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 1940, the Soviet Union forcibly annexed <a href="/wiki/Soviet_occupation_of_the_Baltic_states_(1940)" title="Soviet occupation of the Baltic states (1940)">Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBilinsky19999_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBilinsky19999-23">[23]</a></sup> and the disputed Romanian regions of <a href="/wiki/Soviet_occupation_of_Bessarabia_and_Northern_Bukovina" title="Soviet occupation of Bessarabia and Northern Bukovina">Bessarabia, Northern Bukovina, and Hertza</a>. But after the <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">German Army</a> <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">invaded the Soviet Union</a> in June 1941 and the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">bombed Pearl Harbor</a> in December 1941, the Soviet Union and the Allied powers formed an alliance of convenience. Britain signed a formal alliance and the United States made an informal agreement. In wartime, the United States supplied Britain, the Soviet Union and other Allied nations through its <a href="/wiki/Lend-Lease" title="Lend-Lease">Lend-Lease</a> Program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> However, Stalin remained highly suspicious, and he believed that the British and the Americans had conspired to ensure that the Soviets bore the brunt of the fighting against Germany. According to this view, the Western Allies had deliberately delayed opening a second anti-German front in order to step in at the last minute and shape the peace settlement. Thus, Soviet perceptions of the West left a strong undercurrent of tension and hostility between the Allied powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="End_of_World_War_II_.281945.E2.80.931947.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_World_War_II_(1945&#8211;1947)">End of World War II (1945&#8211;1947)</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wartime_conferences_regarding_post-war_Europe">Wartime conferences regarding post-war Europe</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Tehran_Conference" title="Tehran Conference">Tehran Conference</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yalta_Conference" title="Yalta Conference">Yalta Conference</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Allied_World_War_II_conferences" title="List of Allied World War II conferences">List of Allied World War II conferences</a></div>\n<p>The Allies disagreed about how the European map should look, and how borders would be drawn, following the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis13-23_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis13-23-29">[29]</a></sup> Each side held dissimilar ideas regarding the establishment and maintenance of post-war security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis13-23_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis13-23-29">[29]</a></sup> Some scholars contend that all the Western Allies desired a security system in which democratic governments were established as widely as possible, permitting countries to peacefully resolve differences through <a href="/wiki/International_organization" title="International organization">international organizations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Others note that the Atlantic powers were divided in their vision of the new post-war world. Roosevelt\'s goals&#8212;military victory in both Europe and Asia, the achievement of global American economic supremacy over the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, and the creation of a world peace organization&#8212;were more global than Churchill\'s, which were mainly centered on securing control over the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a>, ensuring the survival of the British Empire, and the independence of Central and Eastern European countries as a <a href="/wiki/Buffer_state" title="Buffer state">buffer</a> between the Soviets and the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plokhy_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plokhy-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yalta_Conference_(Churchill,_Roosevelt,_Stalin)_(B%26W).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2239" data-file-width="2720" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Yalta_Conference_%28Churchill%2C_Roosevelt%2C_Stalin%29_%28B%26W%29.jpg/220px-Yalta_Conference_%28Churchill%2C_Roosevelt%2C_Stalin%29_%28B%26W%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Yalta_Conference_%28Churchill%2C_Roosevelt%2C_Stalin%29_%28B%26W%29.jpg/330px-Yalta_Conference_%28Churchill%2C_Roosevelt%2C_Stalin%29_%28B%26W%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Yalta_Conference_%28Churchill%2C_Roosevelt%2C_Stalin%29_%28B%26W%29.jpg/440px-Yalta_Conference_%28Churchill%2C_Roosevelt%2C_Stalin%29_%28B%26W%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yalta_Conference_(Churchill,_Roosevelt,_Stalin)_(B%26W).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "<a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Big Three</a>" at the Yalta Conference: <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>, 1945</div></div></div>\n<p>The Soviet Union sought to dominate the internal affairs of countries in its border regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis13-23_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis13-23-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> During the war, Stalin had created special training centers for communists from different countries so that they could set up secret police forces loyal to Moscow as soon as the Red Army took control. Soviet agents took control of the media, especially radio; they quickly harassed and then banned all independent civic institutions, from youth groups to schools, churches and rival political parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Stalin also sought continued peace with Britain and the United States, hoping to focus on internal reconstruction and economic growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the American view, Stalin seemed a potential ally in accomplishing their goals, whereas in the British approach Stalin appeared as the greatest threat to the fulfillment of their agenda. With the Soviets already occupying most of Central and Eastern Europe, Stalin was at an advantage, and the two western leaders vied for his favors.\n</p><p>The differences between Roosevelt and Churchill led to several separate deals with the Soviets. In October 1944, Churchill traveled to Moscow and proposed the "<a href="/wiki/Percentages_agreement" title="Percentages agreement">percentages agreement</a>" to divide the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> into respective <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">spheres of influence</a>, including giving Stalin predominance <a href="/wiki/Romania_in_World_War_II" title="Romania in World War II">over Romania</a> and Bulgaria and Churchill carte blanche <a href="/wiki/White_Terror_(Greece)" title="White Terror (Greece)">over Greece.</a> At the <a href="/wiki/Yalta_Conference" title="Yalta Conference">Yalta Conference</a> of February 1945, Roosevelt signed a separate deal with Stalin in regard of Asia and refused to support Churchill on the issues of Poland and the Reparations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plokhy_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plokhy-31">[31]</a></sup> Roosevelt ultimately approved the percentage agreement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> but there was still apparently no firm consensus on the framework for a post-war settlement in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis21_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis21-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map-Germany-1945.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2966" data-file-width="3492" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Map-Germany-1945.svg/220px-Map-Germany-1945.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Map-Germany-1945.svg/330px-Map-Germany-1945.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Map-Germany-1945.svg/440px-Map-Germany-1945.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map-Germany-1945.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Post-war <a href="/wiki/Allied-occupied_Germany" title="Allied-occupied Germany">Allied occupation zones in Germany</a></div></div></div>\n<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Second_Quebec_Conference" title="Second Quebec Conference">Second Quebec Conference</a>, a high-level military conference held in Quebec City, 12&#8211;16 September 1944, Churchill and Roosevelt reached agreement on a number of matters, including a plan for Germany based on <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morgenthau_Jr." title="Henry Morgenthau Jr.">Henry Morgenthau Jr.</a>\'s original proposal. The memorandum drafted by Churchill provided for "eliminating the warmaking industries in the Ruhr and the Saar ... looking forward to <a href="/wiki/Morgenthau_Plan" title="Morgenthau Plan">converting Germany into a country primarily agricultural</a> and pastoral in its character." However, it no longer included a plan to partition the country into several independent states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> On 10 May 1945, President Truman signed the U.S. occupation directive <a href="/wiki/Morgenthau_Plan" title="Morgenthau Plan">JCS 1067</a>, which was in effect for over two years, and was enthusiastically supported by Stalin. It directed the U.S. forces of occupation to "...take no steps looking toward the economic rehabilitation of Germany".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some historians have argued that the Cold War began when <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sunrise_(World_War_II)" title="Operation Sunrise (World War II)">the US negotiated a separate peace</a> with <a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">Nazi SS</a> General <a href="/wiki/Karl_Wolff" title="Karl Wolff">Karl Wolff</a> in northern Italy. The Soviet Union was not allowed to participate and the dispute led to heated correspondence between Franklin Roosevelt and Stalin. General Wolff, a war criminal, appears to have been guaranteed immunity at the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials" title="Nuremberg trials">Nuremberg trials</a> by Office of Strategic Services (<a href="/wiki/Office_of_Strategic_Services" title="Office of Strategic Services">OSS</a>) commander (and later <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a> director) <a href="/wiki/Allen_Dulles" title="Allen Dulles">Allen Dulles</a> when they met in March 1945. Wolff and his forces were being considered to help implement <a href="/wiki/Operation_Unthinkable" title="Operation Unthinkable">Operation Unthinkable</a>, a secret plan to invade the Soviet Union which Winston Churchill advocated during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 1945, President Roosevelt died and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>, who distrusted Stalin and turned for advice to an <a href=\'/wiki/The_Wise_Men_(book)#The_"Wise_Men"\' title="The Wise Men (book)">elite group</a> of foreign policy intellectuals. Both Churchill and Truman opposed, among other things, the Soviets\' decision to prop up the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Committee_of_National_Liberation" title="Polish Committee of National Liberation">Lublin government</a>, the Soviet-controlled rival to the <a href="/wiki/Polish_government-in-exile" title="Polish government-in-exile">Polish government-in-exile</a> in London, whose relations with the Soviets had been severed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/End_of_World_War_II_in_Europe" title="End of World War II in Europe">Allies\' May 1945 victory</a>, the Soviets effectively occupied Central and Eastern Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis21_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis21-37">[37]</a></sup> while strong US and Western allied forces remained in Western Europe. In <a href="/wiki/Allied-occupied_Germany" title="Allied-occupied Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allied-occupied_Austria" title="Allied-occupied Austria">Austria</a>, France, Britain, the Soviet Union and the United States established zones of occupation and a loose framework for parceled four-power control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Conference_on_International_Organization" title="United Nations Conference on International Organization">1945 Allied conference in San Francisco</a> established the multi-national <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> (UN) for the maintenance of <a href="/wiki/World_peace" title="World peace">world peace</a>, but the enforcement capacity of its <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">Security Council</a> was effectively paralyzed by the ability of individual members to exercise <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_veto_power" title="United Nations Security Council veto power">veto power</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Accordingly, the UN was essentially converted into an inactive forum for exchanging polemical rhetoric, and the Soviets regarded it almost exclusively as a propaganda tribune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potsdam_Conference_and_surrender_of_Japan">Potsdam Conference and surrender of Japan</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Conference" title="Potsdam Conference">Potsdam Conference</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">Surrender of Japan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_new_%22Big_Three%22_meet_for_the_first_time_at_the_Potsdam_Conference_in_Potsdam,_Germany._L_to_R,_new_British_Prime..._-_NARA_-_198950.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2428" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/The_new_%22Big_Three%22_meet_for_the_first_time_at_the_Potsdam_Conference_in_Potsdam%2C_Germany._L_to_R%2C_new_British_Prime..._-_NARA_-_198950.jpg/220px-The_new_%22Big_Three%22_meet_for_the_first_time_at_the_Potsdam_Conference_in_Potsdam%2C_Germany._L_to_R%2C_new_British_Prime..._-_NARA_-_198950.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/The_new_%22Big_Three%22_meet_for_the_first_time_at_the_Potsdam_Conference_in_Potsdam%2C_Germany._L_to_R%2C_new_British_Prime..._-_NARA_-_198950.jpg/330px-The_new_%22Big_Three%22_meet_for_the_first_time_at_the_Potsdam_Conference_in_Potsdam%2C_Germany._L_to_R%2C_new_British_Prime..._-_NARA_-_198950.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/The_new_%22Big_Three%22_meet_for_the_first_time_at_the_Potsdam_Conference_in_Potsdam%2C_Germany._L_to_R%2C_new_British_Prime..._-_NARA_-_198950.jpg/440px-The_new_%22Big_Three%22_meet_for_the_first_time_at_the_Potsdam_Conference_in_Potsdam%2C_Germany._L_to_R%2C_new_British_Prime..._-_NARA_-_198950.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_new_%22Big_Three%22_meet_for_the_first_time_at_the_Potsdam_Conference_in_Potsdam,_Germany._L_to_R,_new_British_Prime..._-_NARA_-_198950.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Conference" title="Potsdam Conference">Potsdam Conference</a>, 1945</div></div></div>\n<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Conference" title="Potsdam Conference">Potsdam Conference</a>, which started in late July after Germany\'s surrender, serious differences emerged over the future development of Germany and the rest of Central and Eastern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Byrd_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Byrd-47">[47]</a></sup> Moreover, the participants\' mounting antipathy and bellicose language served to confirm their suspicions about each other\'s hostile intentions, and to entrench their positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> At this conference Truman informed Stalin that the United States possessed a powerful new weapon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis25_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis25-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The US had invited Britain into its atomic bomb project but kept it secret from the Soviet Union. Stalin was aware that the Americans were working on the atomic bomb, and he reacted to the news calmly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis25_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis25-49">[49]</a></sup> One week after the end of the Potsdam Conference, the US <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">bombed Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a>. Shortly after the attacks, Stalin protested to US officials when Truman offered the Soviets little real influence in <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">occupied Japan</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Stalin was also outraged by the actual dropping of the bombs, calling them a &#8220;superbarbarity&#8221; and claiming that &#8220;the balance has been destroyed&#8230;That cannot be.&#8221; The Truman administration intended to use its ongoing nuclear weapons program to pressure the Soviet Union in international relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis25_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis25-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginnings_of_the_Eastern_Bloc">Beginnings of the Eastern Bloc</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Post%E2%80%93World_War_II_economic_expansion" title="Post&#8211;World War II economic expansion">Post&#8211;World War II economic expansion</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="396" decoding="async" height="357" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg/220px-EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg/330px-EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg/440px-EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Post-war territorial changes in Europe and the formation of the Eastern Bloc, the so-called \'Iron Curtain\'</div></div></div>\n<p>During the opening stages of World War II, the Soviet Union laid the foundation for the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> by <a href="/wiki/Military_occupations_by_the_Soviet_Union" title="Military occupations by the Soviet Union">invading and then annexing</a> several countries as <a href="/wiki/Republics_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Republics of the Soviet Union">Soviet Socialist Republics</a>, by agreement with Germany in the <a href="/wiki/Molotov%E2%80%93Ribbentrop_Pact" title="Molotov&#8211;Ribbentrop Pact">Molotov&#8211;Ribbentrop Pact</a>. These included eastern <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> (<a href="/wiki/Territories_of_Poland_annexed_by_the_Soviet_Union" title="Territories of Poland annexed by the Soviet Union">incorporated</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic">Byelorussian SSR</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Ukrainian Soviet Socialist Republic">Ukrainian SSR</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stalinswars43_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-stalinswars43-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> (which became the <a href="/wiki/Latvian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Latvian Soviet Socialist Republic">Latvian SSR</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wettig20_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-wettig20-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senn_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-senn-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> (which became the <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Estonian Soviet Socialist Republic">Estonian SSR</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wettig20_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-wettig20-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senn_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-senn-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> (which became the <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Lithuanian Soviet Socialist Republic">Lithuanian SSR</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wettig20_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-wettig20-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senn_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-senn-53">[53]</a></sup> part of eastern <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> (which became the <a href="/wiki/Karelo-Finnish_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Karelo-Finnish Soviet Socialist Republic">Karelo-Finnish SSR</a>) and eastern <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> (which became the <a href="/wiki/Moldavian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Moldavian Soviet Socialist Republic">Moldavian SSR</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stalinswars55_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-stalinswars55-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shirer794_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-shirer794-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Central and Eastern European territories liberated from Germany and occupied by the Soviet armed forces were added to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> by converting them into <a href="/wiki/Satellite_state" title="Satellite state">satellite states</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitz_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmitz-56">[56]</a></sup> such as:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Flag_of_Albania_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg/21px-Flag_of_Albania_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Flag_of_Albania_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg/32px-Flag_of_Albania_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Flag_of_Albania_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg/42px-Flag_of_Albania_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg.png 2x" width="21"/>&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/People%27s_Socialist_Republic_of_Albania" title="People\'s Socialist Republic of Albania">People\'s Republic of Albania</a></b> (11 January 1946)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cook17_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-cook17-57">[57]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Flag_of_Bulgaria_%281971_%E2%80%93_1990%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_Bulgaria_%281971_%E2%80%93_1990%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Flag_of_Bulgaria_%281971_%E2%80%93_1990%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_Bulgaria_%281971_%E2%80%93_1990%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Flag_of_Bulgaria_%281971_%E2%80%93_1990%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_Bulgaria_%281971_%E2%80%93_1990%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Bulgaria" title="People\'s Republic of Bulgaria">People\'s Republic of Bulgaria</a></b> (15 September 1946)</li>\n<li><b><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Poland_%281928%E2%80%931980%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_Poland_%281928%E2%80%931980%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Poland_%281928%E2%80%931980%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_Poland_%281928%E2%80%931980%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Poland_%281928%E2%80%931980%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_Poland_%281928%E2%80%931980%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Polish_People%27s_Republic" title="Polish People\'s Republic">Polish People\'s Republic</a></b> (19 January 1947)</li>\n<li><b><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_Romania_%281948-1952%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_Romania_%281948-1952%29.svg.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_Romania_%281948-1952%29.svg/45px-Flag_of_Romania_%281948-1952%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_Romania" title="Socialist Republic of Romania">People\'s Republic of Romania</a></b> (13 April 1948)</li>\n<li><b><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg/45px-Flag_of_the_Czech_Republic.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Czechoslovak_Socialist_Republic" title="Czechoslovak Socialist Republic">Czechoslovak Socialist Republic</a></b> (9 May 1948)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Flag_of_Hungary_%281949-1956%3B_1-2_aspect_ratio%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_Hungary_%281949-1956%3B_1-2_aspect_ratio%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Flag_of_Hungary_%281949-1956%3B_1-2_aspect_ratio%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_Hungary_%281949-1956%3B_1-2_aspect_ratio%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Flag_of_Hungary_%281949-1956%3B_1-2_aspect_ratio%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_Hungary_%281949-1956%3B_1-2_aspect_ratio%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Hungarian_People%27s_Republic" title="Hungarian People\'s Republic">Hungarian People\'s Republic</a></b> (20 August 1949)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dijk_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-dijk-59">[59]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Flag_of_East_Germany.svg/23px-Flag_of_East_Germany.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Flag_of_East_Germany.svg/35px-Flag_of_East_Germany.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Flag_of_East_Germany.svg/46px-Flag_of_East_Germany.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">German Democratic Republic</a></b> (7 October 1949)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wettig96_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-wettig96-60">[60]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>The Soviet-style regimes that arose in the Bloc not only reproduced Soviet <a href="/wiki/Planned_economy" title="Planned economy">command economy</a>, but also adopted the brutal methods employed by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> and the Soviet secret police in order to suppress both real and potential opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roht83_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-roht83-61">[61]</a></sup> In Asia, the Red Army had overrun <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> in the last month of the war, and it went on to occupy the large swathe of Korean territory located north of the 38th parallel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As part of consolidating Stalin\'s control over the Eastern Bloc, the <a href="/wiki/NKVD" title="NKVD">People\'s Commissariat for Internal Affairs</a> (NKVD), led by <a href="/wiki/Lavrentiy_Beria" title="Lavrentiy Beria">Lavrentiy Beria</a>, supervised the establishment of Soviet-style secret police systems in the Bloc that were supposed to crush anti-communist resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis_2005,_p._34_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis_2005,_p._34-63">[63]</a></sup> When the slightest stirrings of independence emerged in the Bloc, Stalin\'s strategy matched that of dealing with domestic pre-war rivals: they were removed from power, put on trial, imprisoned, and in several instances, executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis_2005,_p._100_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis_2005,_p._100-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> was concerned that, given the enormous size of Soviet forces deployed in Europe at the end of the war, and the perception that Soviet leader Joseph Stalin was unreliable, there existed a Soviet threat to Western Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph-65">[65]</a></sup> After World War II, US officials guided Western European leaders in establishing their own secret security force to prevent subversion in the Western bloc, which evolved into <a href="/wiki/Operation_Gladio" title="Operation Gladio">Operation Gladio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Containment_and_the_Truman_Doctrine_.281947.E2.80.931953.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Containment_and_the_Truman_Doctrine_(1947&#8211;1953)">Containment and the Truman Doctrine (1947&#8211;1953)</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1947%E2%80%931953)" title="Cold War (1947&#8211;1953)">Cold War (1947&#8211;1953)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Containment" title="Containment">Containment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Truman_Doctrine" title="Truman Doctrine">Truman Doctrine</a></div>\n<h3><span id="The_Iron_Curtain.2C_Iran.2C_Turkey.2C_and_Greece"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Iron_Curtain,_Iran,_Turkey,_and_Greece">The Iron Curtain, Iran, Turkey, and Greece</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/X_Article#The_Long_Telegram" title="X Article">X Article &#167;&#160;The Long Telegram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_Curtain" title="Iron Curtain">Iron Curtain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran_crisis_of_1946" title="Iran crisis of 1946">Iran crisis of 1946</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Restatement_of_Policy_on_Germany" title="Restatement of Policy on Germany">Restatement of Policy on Germany</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_(Zaisa)_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG/220px-%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG/330px-%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG/440px-%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_(Zaisa)_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Remains of the "iron curtain" in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In late February 1946, <a href="/wiki/George_F._Kennan" title="George F. Kennan">George F. Kennan</a>\'s "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_Telegram" title="Long Telegram">Long Telegram</a>" from Moscow to Washington helped to articulate the US government\'s increasingly hard line against the Soviets, which would become the basis for US strategy toward the Soviet Union for the duration of the Cold War. The Truman Administration was receptive to the telegram due to broken promises by Stalin concerning Europe and Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Following the WWII <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Soviet_invasion_of_Iran" title="Anglo-Soviet invasion of Iran">Anglo-Soviet invasion of Iran</a>, the country was occupied by the Red Army in the far north and the British in the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-68">[68]</a></sup> Iran was used by the United States and British to supply the Soviet Union, and the Allies agreed to withdraw from Iran within six months after the cessation of hostilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-68">[68]</a></sup> However, when this deadline came, the Soviets remained in Iran under the guise of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Azerbaijan" title="People\'s Republic of Azerbaijan">People\'s Republic of Azerbaijan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_separatism_in_Iran" title="Kurdish separatism in Iran">Kurdish</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Mahabad" title="Republic of Mahabad">Republic of Mahabad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Shortly thereafter, on March 5, former British prime minister Winston Churchill delivered his famous "<a href="/wiki/Iron_Curtain" title="Iron Curtain">Iron Curtain</a>" speech in <a href="/wiki/Fulton,_Missouri" title="Fulton, Missouri">Fulton, Missouri</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The speech called for an Anglo-American alliance against the Soviets, whom he accused of establishing an "iron curtain" dividing Europe from "<a href="/wiki/Szczecin" title="Szczecin">Stettin</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmitz_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmitz-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A week later, on March 13, Stalin responded vigorously to the speech, saying that Churchill could be compared to <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Hitler</a> insofar as he advocated the racial superiority of <a href="/wiki/List_of_territorial_entities_where_English_is_an_official_language" title="List of territorial entities where English is an official language">English-speaking nations</a> so that they could satisfy their hunger for world domination, and that such a declaration was "a call for war on the U.S.S.R." The Soviet leader also dismissed the accusation that the USSR was exerting increasing control over the countries lying in its sphere. He argued that there was nothing surprising in "the fact that the Soviet Union, anxious for its future safety, [was] trying to see to it that governments loyal in their attitude to the Soviet Union should exist in these countries".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cold_war_europe_military_alliances_map_en.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1165" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Cold_war_europe_military_alliances_map_en.png/200px-Cold_war_europe_military_alliances_map_en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Cold_war_europe_military_alliances_map_en.png/300px-Cold_war_europe_military_alliances_map_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Cold_war_europe_military_alliances_map_en.png/400px-Cold_war_europe_military_alliances_map_en.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">European military alliances</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cold_war_europe_economic_alliances_map_en.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1165" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Cold_war_europe_economic_alliances_map_en.png/200px-Cold_war_europe_economic_alliances_map_en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Cold_war_europe_economic_alliances_map_en.png/300px-Cold_war_europe_economic_alliances_map_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Cold_war_europe_economic_alliances_map_en.png/400px-Cold_war_europe_economic_alliances_map_en.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">European economic alliances</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>In September, the Soviet side produced the <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Vasilevich_Novikov" title="Nikolai Vasilevich Novikov">Novikov</a> telegram, sent by the Soviet ambassador to the US but commissioned and "co-authored" by <a href="/wiki/Vyacheslav_Molotov" title="Vyacheslav Molotov">Vyacheslav Molotov</a>; it portrayed the US as being in the grip of monopoly capitalists who were building up military capability "to prepare the conditions for winning world supremacy in a new war".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> On September 6, 1946, <a href="/wiki/James_F._Byrnes" title="James F. Byrnes">James F. Byrnes</a> delivered a <a href="/wiki/Restatement_of_Policy_on_Germany" title="Restatement of Policy on Germany">speech</a> in Germany repudiating the <a href="/wiki/Morgenthau_Plan" title="Morgenthau Plan">Morgenthau Plan</a> (a proposal to partition and de-industrialize post-war Germany) and warning the Soviets that the US intended to maintain a military presence in Europe indefinitely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> As Byrnes admitted a month later, "The nub of our program was to win the German people&#160;... it was a battle between us and Russia over minds&#160;..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In December, the Soviets agreed to withdraw from Iran after persistent US pressure, an early success of containment policy.\n</p><p>By 1947, US president <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> was outraged by perceived resistance of the Soviet Union to American demands in Iran, Turkey, and Greece, as well as Soviet rejection of the <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Plan" title="Baruch Plan">Baruch Plan</a> on nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In February 1947, the British government announced that it could no longer afford to finance the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Greece_(Gl%C3%BCcksburg)" title="Kingdom of Greece (Gl&#252;cksburg)">Kingdom of Greece</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greek_Civil_War" title="Greek Civil War">its civil war</a> against Communist-led insurgents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">US government</a> responded to this announcement by adopting a policy of <a href="/wiki/Containment" title="Containment">containment</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis28_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis28-79">[79]</a></sup> with the goal of stopping the spread of Communism. Truman delivered a speech calling for the allocation of $400 million to intervene in the war and unveiled the <a href="/wiki/Truman_Doctrine" title="Truman Doctrine">Truman Doctrine</a>, which framed the conflict as a contest between free peoples and totalitarian regimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaddis28_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddis28-79">[79]</a></sup> American policymakers accused the Soviet Union of conspiring against the Greek royalists in an effort to <a href="/wiki/Domino_theory" title="Domino theory">expand Soviet influence</a> even though Stalin had told the Communist Party to cooperate with the British-backed government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> (The insurgents were helped by <a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Josip Broz Tito</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia</a> against Stalin\'s wishes.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lafeber_1993_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lafeber_1993-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Enunciation of the Truman Doctrine marked the beginning of a US bipartisan defense and foreign policy consensus between <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republicans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrats</a> focused on containment and <a href="/wiki/Deterrence_theory" title="Deterrence theory">deterrence</a> that weakened during and after the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, but ultimately persisted thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Moderate and conservative parties in Europe, as well as social democrats, gave virtually unconditional support to the Western alliance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Eurocommunism" title="Eurocommunism">European</a> and <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_USA" title="Communist Party USA">American Communists</a>, financed by the <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> and involved in its intelligence operations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> adhered to Moscow\'s line, although dissent began to appear after 1956. Other critiques of the consensus policy came from <a href="/wiki/Opposition_to_United_States_involvement_in_the_Vietnam_War" title="Opposition to United States involvement in the Vietnam War">anti-Vietnam War activists</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Nuclear_Disarmament" title="Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament">Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">anti-nuclear movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Marshall_Plan_and_Czechoslovak_coup_d.27.C3.A9tat"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Marshall_Plan_and_Czechoslovak_coup_d\'&#233;tat">Marshall Plan and Czechoslovak coup d\'&#233;tat</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Bloc" title="Western Bloc">Western Bloc</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1948_Czechoslovak_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1948 Czechoslovak coup d\'&#233;tat">1948 Czechoslovak coup d\'&#233;tat</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US-MarshallPlanAid-Logo.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="608" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/US-MarshallPlanAid-Logo.svg/200px-US-MarshallPlanAid-Logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/US-MarshallPlanAid-Logo.svg/300px-US-MarshallPlanAid-Logo.svg.png 1.5x,
